    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer2469436478368106956">This is the story of Moses Herzog, a great sufferer, joker, mourner, and charmer. Although his life steadily disintegrates around him - he has failed as a writer and teacher, as a father, and has lost the affection of his wife to his best friend - Herzog sees himself as a survivor, both of his private disasters and those of the age. He writes unsent letters to friends and </span>
  <span id="freeText2469436478368106956" style="display:none">This is the story of Moses Herzog, a great sufferer, joker, mourner, and charmer. Although his life steadily disintegrates around him - he has failed as a writer and teacher, as a father, and has lost the affection of his wife to his best friend - Herzog sees himself as a survivor, both of his private disasters and those of the age. He writes unsent letters to friends and enemies, colleagues and famous people, revealing his wry perception of the world around him, and the innermost secrets of his heart.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1694852-why-is-herzog-considered-a-great-novel-i">Why is Herzog considered a great novel? I enjoyed it, though it did seem to sag in the middle then pick up in the last third to quarter.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1694852'><a id="like_count_community_question_1694852" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1694852?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F6551.Herzog">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1513962691i/39346323._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'd say it's structured beautifully (the letter writing) or maybe how we never really understand how Herzog is any different from anyone else in the w<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'd say it's structured beautifully (the letter writing) or maybe how we never really understand how Herzog is any different from anyone else in the way of pain/illness, or how the end is pitch perfect. But I'd also have to add it's just beautifully written, up there in the realm of Updike and Nabokov.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_327539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/327539" />
    <a title="Dave Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26185-dave-russell"><img alt="Dave Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1256777558p2/26185.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/327539?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dave Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dave Russell" href="/user/show/26185-dave-russell">Dave Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26185-dave-russell?shelf=worst">worst</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26185-dave-russell?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26185-dave-russell?shelf=jewish-literature">jewish-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer327539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13019697235044508415"><i>Dear Saul,<br/>I'm afraid it's over. I can no longer have you on my favorite authors list. </i>(No, no let go of F. Scott's sleeve. You're only making this harder than it needs to be.)<i> I want to tell you how much I loved Henderson the Rain King. One of my favorites. It was so full of wit and energy. Then I had to go and read this piece of crap, Herzog. Whereas Henderson was an adventure, this was just a big long bitch session. </i>(Hey, give Borges back his cane.)<i> Yes, fine maybe it's me. In fact I'm sure it</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13019697235044508415" style="display:none"><i>Dear Saul,<br />I'm afraid it's over. I can no longer have you on my favorite authors list. </i>(No, no let go of F. Scott's sleeve. You're only making this harder than it needs to be.)<i> I want to tell you how much I loved Henderson the Rain King. One of my favorites. It was so full of wit and energy. Then I had to go and read this piece of crap, Herzog. Whereas Henderson was an adventure, this was just a big long bitch session. </i>(Hey, give Borges back his cane.)<i> Yes, fine maybe it's me. In fact I'm sure it is. Just like songs with melodies I can easily whistle, I need books with plots where things happen. I'm just not smart enough to be satisfied with the philosophy laden interior monologues that comprise most of this book (416 pages, for chrissakes!) I mean I can't make heads or tails of this passage:</i><br /><br />Whereas a man like me has shown the arbitrary withdrawal of proud subjectivity from the collective and historical progress of mankind. And that is true of lower-class boys and girls who adopt the aesthetic mode, the mode of rich sensibility. Seeking to sustain their own version of existen...<br /><br /><i>And it just goes on like that. Sorry I couldn't bring myself to type the rest of that paragraph. So it's me. I'm obviously not wise enough for this book. But still you gotta go. </i>(And don't steal Nabokov's pen on the way out. Could someone send Dostoyevsky in here, please?) <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="13019697235044508415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16779118669183015901">Herzog is one of Bellow's most enduring characters and this is one of his best books. When not screwing up his life, his letters to people real, dead and imaginary kept me laughing the entire time. I loved how despite everything, there is a feeling of exuberance about life in this book and it made me want to go back and read it again in a year. Highly entertaining, there is never a dull moment and lots and lots of humor. I would say for those that have not read Bellow that this is an excellent j</span>
  <span id="freeText16779118669183015901" style="display:none">Herzog is one of Bellow's most enduring characters and this is one of his best books. When not screwing up his life, his letters to people real, dead and imaginary kept me laughing the entire time. I loved how despite everything, there is a feeling of exuberance about life in this book and it made me want to go back and read it again in a year. Highly entertaining, there is never a dull moment and lots and lots of humor. I would say for those that have not read Bellow that this is an excellent jumping off point before, say, Augie March, Henderson the Rain King, Sammler's Planet...a must read! Having now read almost all of Bellow's novels, Herzog still stands out as one of my favorites.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5092672265299119573">Herzog, Saul Bellow<br/><br/><b>Herzog</b> is a 1964 novel by <b>Saul Bellow</b>. It won the U.S. National Book Award for Fiction and the Prix International. In 2005, Time magazine named it one of the 100 best novels in the English language since Time's founding in 1923.<br/><br/><b>Herzog</b> is set in 1964 in the United States, and is about the midlife crisis of a Jewish man named <b>Moses E.   <span id="freeText5092672265299119573" style="display:none">Herzog, Saul Bellow<br /><br /><b>Herzog</b> is a 1964 novel by <b>Saul Bellow</b>. It won the U.S. National Book Award for Fiction and the Prix International. In 2005, Time magazine named it one of the 100 best novels in the English language since Time's founding in 1923.<br /><br /><b>Herzog</b> is set in 1964 in the United States, and is about the midlife crisis of a Jewish man named <b>Moses E. Herzog</b>. At the age of forty-seven, he is just emerging from his second divorce, this one particularly acrimonious. He has two children, one by each wife, who are growing up without him. His career as a writer and an academic has foundered. He is in a relationship with a vibrant woman, Ramona, but finds himself running away from commitment.<br /><br />Herzog's second marriage, to the demanding, manipulative Madeleine, has recently ended in a humiliating fashion. While still actively married, Madeleine convinced Moses to move her and their daughter Junie to Chicago, and to arrange for their best friends, Valentine and Phoebe Gersbach, to move as well, securing a solid job for Valentine. However, the plans were all a ruse, as Madeleine and Valentine were carrying on an affair behind Moses's back, and shortly after arriving in Chicago, Madeleine throws Herzog out, secures a restraining order against him, and attempts to have him committed to an asylum.<br /><br />Herzog spends much of his time mentally writing letters he never sends. These letters are aimed at friends, family members, and famous figures. The recipients may be dead, and Herzog has often never met them. The one common thread is that Herzog is always expressing disappointment, either his own in the failings of others or their words, or apologizing for the way he has disappointed others. ...<br /><br />عنوانها: هرتزوک؛ هرتسوگ؛ تاریخ نخستی خوانش: روز پنجم ماه ژوئن سال 2001میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: هرتزوک؛ نویسنده: سال بلو؛ مترجم: فرشته داوران؛ تهران، پیکان، 1379؛ در 520ص؛ شابک 9643281019؛ <br /><br />عنوان: هرتزوک؛ نویسنده: سال بلو؛ مترجم: فریدون رضوانیه؛ تهران، انتشارات پویان ، 1395، در 408ص؛ شابک 9786009538423؛ <br /><br />عنوان: هرتسوگ؛ نویسنده: سال بلو؛ مترجم: مهداد ایرانی طلب؛ تهران، مهد مینا، 1379، در 559ص؛ شابک 9646915086؛<br /><br />هرتزوگ، مردی میانسال، و یک استاد دانشگاه، روشنفکر، و دارای اندیشه های والاست، که همسر بسیار زیبا، و جوانش «مادلین»، به تازگی از او جدا شده، و معشوقه ی دوست صمیمی او «والنتاین»، شده است؛ این موضوع شوک عاطفی بزرگی به «هرتزوگ» وارد نموده، و تا حدودی او را از حالت تعادل خارج کرده است؛ در این شرایط جدید، «هرتزوگ» شروع به نامه نوشتن میکند، و بحثها، و استدلالهای خود را، در نامه ها بیان میکند؛ این نامه ها خطاب به «مادلین»، «دوستانش»، «همکارانش»، «فامیلش»، «افراد فوت شده»، «دانشمندان مشهور»، «رئیس جمهور» و ....؛هستند؛ هر چند نامه ها هیچکدام پست نمیشوند؛ نامه ها از سوی مردی شکست خورده، فریب داده شده، و در اوج ناامیدی نوشته شده اند، و «هرتزوگ» دائما در حین نوشتن نامه ها، با رسیدن به یک جمله، یا کلمه ی ویژه ای، تمرکز در نوشتن را از دست میدهد، و به دنبال یادمانهای خویش میرود، و در بین یادمانها متوجه میشود، باید به شخص دیگری نامه بنویسد؛ «هرتزوگ» که «استاد فلسفه»، و «زبان» است، برای حل مشکلش نمیتواند از دانش خود سود برد؛ و...؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="5092672265299119573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_49725938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49725938" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4695-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1264806364p2/4695.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49725938?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/4695-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/3961517-herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4695-daniel?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49725938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12560945905991368439">During the time I was reading "Herzog," NPR coincidentally ran one of its "You Must Read This" pieces, this one by Jeffrey Eugenides and touting Saul Bellow's novel. In the piece, Eugenides says:<br/><blockquote>There's a little thing I do when I can't write: When I'm feeling sleepy, when my head is in a fog, I reach across my desk, digging under the piles of unanswered mail, to unearth my copy of "Herzog" by Saul Bellow. And then I open the book — anywhere — and read a paragraph.<br/><br/>It always works. Right away I'm </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12560945905991368439" style="display:none">During the time I was reading "Herzog," NPR coincidentally ran one of its "You Must Read This" pieces, this one by Jeffrey Eugenides and touting Saul Bellow's novel. In the piece, Eugenides says:<br /><blockquote>There's a little thing I do when I can't write: When I'm feeling sleepy, when my head is in a fog, I reach across my desk, digging under the piles of unanswered mail, to unearth my copy of "Herzog" by Saul Bellow. And then I open the book — anywhere — and read a paragraph.<br /><br />It always works. Right away I'm restored to full alertness and clarity. Style, in literature, has gone out of style. People think it's just ornament. But it's not: The work that goes into a writer's style, the choices that are taken, the cliches that are chucked, represent a refining of thought and feeling into their purest, most intelligent, most moral form.</blockquote><br />The danger with great stylists such as Bellow, Eugenides says, is that the style can overshadow the substance. Bellow, he argues, successfully avoids this potential pitfall. "His sentences pack maximum sensual, emotional and intellectual information into minimum space — all the while generating an involving, deeply moving story." Eugenides goes on to say:<br /><blockquote>The impulse here is to quote. Every single page of "Herzog" teems with jokes, apercus, deep-thinker riffs — little genius moves every other sentence. The impulse is to read the entire book out loud.</blockquote><br />I quote Eugenides at length here because I think he hits the nail right on the head. While "Herzog" is the only Bellow book I've read so far (Eugenides, in the New York Times, called it "the best of Bellow’s great novels") I feel I can already say that he's one of the great ones — one of the great 20th century prose stylists, along with such writers as Nabokov. (I single out Nabokov mostly because I just read "Pnin" for the first time a few weeks back, and find him to be another novelist, like Bellow, who truly loves and makes the most of the English language.) Just as I plan to read more Nabokov almost solely for his prose style, I'll be reading more Bellow as well.<br /><br />So why four stars rather than five for "Herzog?" While Bellow's prose style is wonderful, and serves the story well and the character of Moses Herzog perfectly, the story itself felt a bit thin at times. That caused the novel, even at just 350 pages, to be a bit of a slog to get through at some points. (And I say this even though I was reading the almost 1,500-page "Count of Monte Cristo" at the same time. It'd be an overstatement to say that book seemed shorter than "Herzog," but they perhaps felt close to the same length.)<br /><br />"Herzog" is nevertheless worth reading by anyone who enjoys great prose stylists — writers who know how to match their style to their story, while avoiding the use of verbal gymnastics simply to show off. Such writers are a rare find.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12560945905991368439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2559760830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13199508302902895422">This was the book that started me reading everything this Nobel Prize-winning American had written till then...<br/><br/>It was one gorgeous summer in the sixties, in the back yard, sprawled lazily in the bright blue and red plastic hammock, oblivious to work, mosquitoes and rain clouds! <br/><br/>I remember reading Norman Mailer’s Armies of the Night not long afterward. Yes - it was THAT summer - 1968.<br/><br/>George McGovern was the Dark Horse Democratic nominee in the Presidential election. The opposing Republican candid</span>
  <span id="freeText13199508302902895422" style="display:none">This was the book that started me reading everything this Nobel Prize-winning American had written till then...<br /><br />It was one gorgeous summer in the sixties, in the back yard, sprawled lazily in the bright blue and red plastic hammock, oblivious to work, mosquitoes and rain clouds! <br /><br />I remember reading Norman Mailer’s Armies of the Night not long afterward. Yes - it was THAT summer - 1968.<br /><br />George McGovern was the Dark Horse Democratic nominee in the Presidential election. The opposing Republican candidate? Richard Nixon. ‘Nuff said...<br /><br />So it was another Long Hot Summer of riots, tear gas and anger. Herzog was my only escape.<br /><br />And I was riveted to the story - as it should be.<br /><br />Don’t know Bellow? <br /><br />Sure you do! <br /><br />He’s your uncle Sid, who grabs you by the lapel and snickers, ‘Listen up, kid! This is a real doozy. Have I got a story to tell YOU, son!’ <br /><br />And holds you breathless till he’s reached his riotous punch line.<br /><br />Except there’s no punch line in Herzog - or rather, the punch line dawns on you only after you’ve been reading it a while. <br /><br />Like being taken on that awful first trip down the garden path, only to come to the first sudden ‘UH-oh’....<br /><br />It’s masterfully woven into Moses Herzog’s obsession with writing letters - endlessly. <br /><br />Again, enough said.<br /><br />But this book took America by storm back in the Kennedy era, and I was now hearkening back to that time of hope, and catching up.<br /><br />Like Uncle Sid, it grabbed the literate nation by the lapels and said, ‘Look, Son - this ain’t a book - it’s LIFE itself, endlessly unrolling in new and unpredictable ways before your very eyes! Just keep your eyes on the bouncing ball - this could be you...’<br /><br />How true are these words! <br /><br />For if you follow Uncle Sid’s argument to the end, you’ll fall as into a Vortex. And from that vortex there’s no escape.<br /><br />It’s like the characters in Patrick White’s Eye of the Storm. <br /><br />As they say in the ancient myth, once you see the full face of a Gorgon you die. <br /><br />And, as you probably remember, each character in White’s wonderful novel is slowly putting that complete Face together in their frantic minds...<br /><br />Once Herzog sees that Gorgon, he too dies, figuratively.<br /><br />But you know WHAT else?<br /><br />Unless a seed DIES it cannot be reborn. <br /><br />So each of us, like a seed, must die AGAIN AND AGAIN to this crazy world.<br /><br />Without hope and trust, though - you won’t die, but burn. With hope and trust, you will regain the COURAGE to Live.<br /><br />Again and again. <br /><br />Until all the living’s done and all that’s left is LOVE.<br /><br />Yes, this is a book of Life. And freedom. <br /><br />And the NECESSARY risks of living in freedom - WITHOUT brutal coercion.<br /><br />AND... this is a book with HEART. <br /><br />A Heart that reveals the Eternal Sacrifice that Love entails.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13199508302902895422" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3138155253" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3138155253" />
    <a title="MihaElla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36408043-mihaella"><img alt="MihaElla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596985417p2/36408043.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3138155253?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MihaElla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MihaElla" href="/user/show/36408043-mihaella">MihaElla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/50092103-herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3138155253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12690572015271399687">Professor Moses Elkanah Herzog as main actor in ‘Herzog’ reminds me a great deal about another chief character, Ricardo Reis of Jose Saramago from ‘The Year of the Death of Ricardo Reis’, both high-minded intellectuals, graduated and with an strong inclination towards philosophy, although it looks much more appropriate to M.E. Herzog than to Ricardo Reis who is a doctor by education and profession. Both of them enjoy rummaging, soberly deliberating within their own minds, losing themselves in wr</span>
  <span id="freeText12690572015271399687" style="display:none">Professor Moses Elkanah Herzog as main actor in ‘Herzog’ reminds me a great deal about another chief character, Ricardo Reis of Jose Saramago from ‘The Year of the Death of Ricardo Reis’, both high-minded intellectuals, graduated and with an strong inclination towards philosophy, although it looks much more appropriate to M.E. Herzog than to Ricardo Reis who is a doctor by education and profession. Both of them enjoy rummaging, soberly deliberating within their own minds, losing themselves in writing, mostly mentally with Herzog and by written hand with Reis, lots of letters, poems, poetry, lines that sound very rich, apparently in substance, but not necessarily to lead to any practical meaning.<br />I found my first novel by Saul Bellow very savoury, mostly it made me laugh, although it’s not funny at all, as standing from an objective perspective it treats decently serious life situations. <br /><br />≪ Well, there is a piece of famous advice, grand advice even if it is German, to forget what you can’t bear. The strong can forget, can shut out history. Very good! Even if it is self-flattery to speak of strength – these aesthetic philosophers, they take a posture, but power sweeps postures away. Still, it’s true you can’t go on transposing one nightmare into another, Nietzsche was certainly right about that. The tender-minded must harden themselves. Is this world nothing but a barren lump of coke? No, no, but what sometimes seems a system of prevention, a denial of what every human being knows. <br />I love my children, but I am the world to them, and bring them nightmares. I had this child by my enemy. And I love her. The sight of her, the odor of her hair, this minute, makes me tremble with love. Isn’t it mysterious how I love the child of my enemy? But a man doesn’t need happiness for himself. No, he can put up with any amount of torment – with recollections, with his own familiar evils, despair. And this is the unwritten history of man, his unseen, negative accomplishment, his power to do without gratification for himself provided there is something great, something into which his being, and all being, can go. He does not need meaning as long as such intensity has scope. Because then it is self-evident; it is meaning. ≫<br /><br />≪ No, the good in his heart evidently didn’t count for much, for here, at the age of forty-seven, he was coming home after a night out with a lip made sore by biting and kissing, his problems as unsolved as ever, and what else did he have to show for himself at the bar of judgment? He had had two wives; there were two children; he had once been a scholar, and in the closet his old valise was swelled like a scaly crocodile with his uncompleted manuscript. Two years ago a Berkeley professor name Mermelstein had scooped him, confounding, overwhelming stunning everyone in the field, as Herzog had meant to do. Mermelstein was a clever man, and an excellent scholar. At least he must be free from personal drama and able to give the world an example of order, thus deserving a place in the human community. But he, Herzog, had committed a sin of some kind against his own heart, while in pursuit of a grand synthesis. What a catalogue of errors!! ≫<br /><br />≪ He tried to make his lust comical, to show how absurd it all was, easily the most wretched form of human struggle, the very essence of slavery. ≫<br /><br />≪ Evidently I continue to believe in God. Though never admitting it. But what else explains my conduct and my life? So I may as well acknowledge how things are, if only because otherwise I can’t even be described. My behavior implies that there is a barrier against which I have been pressing from the first, pressing all my life, with the conviction that it is necessary to press, and that something must come of it. Perhaps that I can eventually pass through. I must always have had such an idea. Is it faith? Or is it simply childishness, expecting to be loved for doing your bidden task? It is, if you’re looking for the psychological explanation, childish and classically depressive. But Herzog didn’t believe that the harshest or most niggardly explanation, following the law of parsimony, was necessarily the truest. Eager impulses, love, intensity, passionate dizziness that make a man sick. How long can I stand such inner beating? The front wall of this body will go down. My whole life beating against the boundaries, and the force of balked longings coming back as stinging poison. Evil, evil, evil…! Excited, characteristic, ecstatic love turning to evil.≫<br /><br />≪ The dream of man’s heart, however much we may distrust and resent it, is that life may complete itself in significant pattern. Some incomprehensible way. Before death. Not irrationally but incomprehensibly fulfilled. You get one last chance to know justice. Truth! ≫<br /><br />I am in absolute agreement with Mr Moses E Herzog; there is absolutely too much nonsense in the world. So, apparently the recipe for enjoying a great(er) freedom is to take life in a very simple, non-theological way. Then there are no surprises as there are no problems. Everything is/will be a mystery, but not a problem, as one will just live for the mystery, and not solving problems. <br />Herzog is doing a lot of “mental” exploration. He should better do meditation instead. That will take him towards an exploration of the mystery, and not searching to find solutions to whatever problems. But are there any problems? He would like to dissolve slowly, slowly. Where to? Anywhere, but not into nonsense, at least this is what he tells himself. <br />He is feeling too much and too strongly. He has lots of desires, thus he is always out of incompletion. He would like to feel (and think) as an emperor, but he practically feels like a beggar. That’s why all the agitation that leads him to a horror of living with some many dangerous results – a failure in his private life, not very successful in his public life, what else remains?<br />Herzog seems to represent through his philosophy the ancient idea of the man as serious, sad, miserable, suffering. This earth is seen as a punishment, he is imprisoned here – and how he can be joyous when life itself is condemned as punishment? Then the only way out of miseries is to get rid of life, and especially of that part of life that damage the mental or emotional side of being.<br />There is a whole rubbish of the past. His life is an indicative of that too. He has to fight life, to fight everything that makes life beautiful, joyous, everything that makes him desirous of life. He is a victim, just as everybody else in the world is a victim. On the one hand he was told to be anti-life, that being the ancient idea of renunciation, towards the world and its joys. But he cannot help it. On the other hand, he was told to sacrifice himself for others. To think of oneself, was condemned as selfish. He is claimed that he should think of others, to think of others’ well-being, of others’ freedom.<br />While reading the book, mostly at work (again, against all the company's rules, but fortunately there are some nice phone booths in the office which can allow full privacy, especially for reading), I started to gather some texts I liked but then I gave up, it is nonsense, I should quote almost whole book. Anyway, here is just a small sample.<br /><br />Conclusion: I really like philosophers. But only while reading them or about them, otherwise there is a strong conflict. I seem to have been born a natural philosopher, too.<br /><br />*Herzog himself had no small amount of charm. But his sexual powers had been damaged by Madeleine. And without the ability to attract women, how was he to recover? It was in this respect that he felt most like a convalescent. The paltriness of these sexual struggles.<br />*Unless you are utterly exploded, there is always something to be grateful for. <br />*His eccentricities had him in their power.<br />*Man’s life is not a business.<br />*Beauty is not a human invention.<br />*His achievements were not only scholarly but sexual. And were those achievements? It was his pride that must be satisfied. His flesh got what was left over.<br />*She was not young; probably in her thirties. (goodness, what a prejudice!)<br />*Instead of answering, he wrote mentally… <br />*Heartsore? Yes, he further wrote. But my vanity will no longer give me much mileage and to tell you the truth I’m not even greatly impressed with my own tortured heart. It begins to seem another waste of time.<br />*You have to fight for your life. That’s the chief condition on which you hold it. Then why be half-hearted? <br />*A sexual reflex that had nothing to do with age or subtlety, wisdom, experience, history, etc.. In sickness or health there came the old quack-quack at the fragrance of perfumed, feminine skin.<br />*For Christ’s sake, don’t cry, you idiot! Live or die, but don’t poison everything.<br />*It hardly does much good to have a complex mind without actually being a philosopher.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12690572015271399687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_457219789" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/457219789" />
    <a title="AC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1655437-ac"><img alt="AC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228702469p2/1655437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/457219789?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="AC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="AC" href="/user/show/1655437-ac">AC</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1655437-ac?shelf=saul-bellow">saul-bellow</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1655437-ac?shelf=novels-english">novels-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer457219789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10283370376792503647">This book has warts – oh, does it have warts…! Like Moses Herzog himself, this book is marred and marked with warts…. But it is a book of genius nonetheless – and not just in parts, but in whole – in scope and in depth…. <br/><br/>I rarely write reviews about fiction – I’m not a literary type. One of the very few I’ve written worth reading is that of The Sun Also Rises. Fiction is not amenable to the type of analysis that comes most naturally to me. <br/><br/>Besides, I’ve only been reading fiction, after a long hi</span>
  <span id="freeText10283370376792503647" style="display:none">This book has warts – oh, does it have warts…! Like Moses Herzog himself, this book is marred and marked with warts…. But it is a book of genius nonetheless – and not just in parts, but in whole – in scope and in depth…. <br /><br />I rarely write reviews about fiction – I’m not a literary type. One of the very few I’ve written worth reading is that of The Sun Also Rises. Fiction is not amenable to the type of analysis that comes most naturally to me. <br /><br />Besides, I’ve only been reading fiction, after a long hiatus, for a year or two -- so really…, what’s to say?<br /><br />But since most of my GR friends who liked this book have not commented on it; while those who have, mostly don’t like it – I feel I need to say something in defense of poor Herzog.<br /><br />When I started this, I had the sinking feeling I had when I tried to read Henderson, The Rain King – many, many years ago. Something just didn’t click. Bellow’s names sound (indeed, they *are*) artificial – something that bugs me no end in a book…; and it’s hard to construct a universe in the opening pages, in any event – so that one has doubts early on…. And so, I almost gave up. <br /><br />But that would have been a great mistake. For this is a large book, a book written ‘in grandi dimensioni’.<br /><br />Andre Gide (I think it was) commented about Dostoevsky that, in his novels, ideas became flesh. He was thinking of The Brothers Karamazov, as I recall. And indeed, of the postwar American novelists, Bellow perhaps comes closest to this – as his characters not only ‘represent’ ideas, but utterly *live* them, engage them, struggle with them, breath them, exude them… – not only intellectually (though that, of course!), but also as LIVED maxims, as lived DILEMMAS… It is a philosophy of life, he wants… a philosophy FOR life that he seeks – one steeped in our historical moment, of course, because the hallmark of modernity is, after all, its historicity…. And by this enormously ambitious standard, Bellow – and already the Bellow of Herzog – succeeds admirably, brilliantly, convincingly… in bringing modern, urban, cosmopolitan – that is, Jewish intellectuals onto the tragicomic stage that was, perhaps, in one sense – that of the hyper-learned, sensate, quivering, irreverent, sexualized, incandescent, doubting, longing intellectual of late modernity – that of Freud made flesh in the bookstalls of the Upper West Side – the peak and apex of Western modernity -- a modernity, indeed, a West..., now in terminal decline.<br /><br />Well… what can I say…? I wax nostalgic….<br /><br />But a rich and wonderful book…. warts and all.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10283370376792503647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_150036398" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150036398" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150036398?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141184876" href="/book/show/1029112.Herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=bellow">bellow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150036398" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4299226295275040458"><b>The Noble Lion</b><br/><br/>Moses Herzog is an academic, an individual who is used to seeing himself as a prince, a noble, a patrician, a patriarch. He's not a plebeian. He's not upwardly mobile. He believes he's already at the peak. He's somebody who stands out from the crowd. He has dignity.<br/><br/>He displays <i>"the pride of the peacock, the lust of the goat, and the wrath of the lion."</i> Of these three characteristics, the most significant is that he is leonine (the ultimate compliment Saul Bellow would ever pay anyo</span>
  <span id="freeText4299226295275040458" style="display:none"><b>The Noble Lion</b><br /><br />Moses Herzog is an academic, an individual who is used to seeing himself as a prince, a noble, a patrician, a patriarch. He's not a plebeian. He's not upwardly mobile. He believes he's already at the peak. He's somebody who stands out from the crowd. He has dignity.<br /><br />He displays <i>"the pride of the peacock, the lust of the goat, and the wrath of the lion."</i> Of these three characteristics, the most significant is that he is leonine (the ultimate compliment Saul Bellow would ever pay anyone, as he did William Gaddis), a king of the jungle.<br /><br />Herzog is ensconced in the world of culture, ideas, ideologies, philosophy and metaphysics. He has published a well-received monograph entitled <i>"Romanticism and Christianity".</i> However, his career seems to have stalled, at least partly as a result of tensions in his personal relationships:<br /><br /><i>"I am a specialist in spiritual self-awareness; or emotionalism; or ideas; or nonsense."</i> <br /><br /><b>The Broken-Down Monarch</b><br /><br />By the time we meet Herzog, he has been married twice (Daisy and Madeleine), divorced once and now separated a second time, as the result of his cuckolding by a former close friend, the poet and broadcaster, Valentine Gersbach. He has become a <i>"broken-down monarch".</i> <br /><br />There's no reason to suspect that Herzog has been faithful during either marriage. It's unlikely that this lion would have been content with just one lioness. However, Herzog totally freaks out when he learns of Madeleine's infidelity.<br /><br />Apparently, the affair had been going on for some time previously. What is most hurtful is that it seems that everybody knew about it but Herzog.<br /><br />Herzog paints a flattering portrait of Madeleine:<br /><br /><i>"She is a beauty, and a very rare type, too, because she is so brilliant."</i><br /><br />She has a Ph.D. in Russian religious history and has mothered Herzog's daughter. He really did love her passionately, at least in the beginning.<br /><br /><b>Listen to the Lioness</b><br /><br />Over the course of the novel, we learn the very simple nature of Madeleine's dissatisfaction: Herzog just didn't listen to her enough. He wanted to be the star who shone brightest in every sky above their heads. She, understandably, wanted to shine as well.<br /><br />Ultimately, for all the intellectualism and sexuality that they shared, their relationship was just too internally competitive to survive, at least on the rules that they set collectively or individually.<br /><br />Madeleine ejects Herzog from the family home in Chicago, some time around October. Most of the action takes place the following May and June, around the time that the academic year has finished and Herzog is trying to deal with his estrangement, both emotionally and intellectually.<br /><br />There is no prospect of Herzog returning to Madeleine, as there was that Odysseus would return to Helen or Leopold Bloom would return to Molly.<br /><br />Herzog has been rejected, but at the same time set free.<br /><br /><b>Blows against the Empire</b><br /><br />This is an enormous blow to Herzog's vanity. It unhinges him so much that Madeleine and Valentine spread rumours that he is insane or has at the very least had a nervous breakdown.<br /><br />Herzog even entertains the thought himself. In one of the most famous first sentences, he says:<br /><br /><i>"If I am out of my mind, it's all right with me."</i><br /><br />Thus, Bellow immediately puts us on notice that the sanity of his protagonist is an issue. (It's interesting that, at this point, the impact of his insanity on family and friends is of no apparent concern to him.)<br /><br />Herzog visits the doctor who can find nothing wrong with him physically:<br /><br /><i>"The strength of Herzog's constitution worked obstinately against his hypochondria...He had been hoping for some definite sickness which would send him to a hospital for a while. He would not have to look after himself."</i><br /><br />Herzog is just over-excited. He has disintegrated metaphysically. He needs a rest. A stay in hospital would mean that he could cease being responsible for himself. <i>"His egotism [is] in abeyance, all converted into passivity."</i><br /><br /><b>Ridiculous Scribbling</b><br /><br />Herzog spends much of the novel venting his spleen in the form of letters to all and sundry. He could almost have been the first blogger or troll.<br /><br />Initially, these letters and the need to write them reflect the work of a madman. In retrospect, they are attempts to reconstruct his life and worldview. He has experienced a meltdown. Now he needs to reboot and reconfigure. He isn't mad, just egotistical and eccentric:<br /><br /><i>"He knew his scribbling, his letter-writing, was ridiculous. It was involuntary. His eccentricities had him in their power."</i><br /><br />The novel is written primarily in the third person. This gives the reader the opportunity to see a perspective beyond that of Herzog. However, often the narrative dealing with Herzog's perspective slips into the first person. Bellow is always in control. However, he subsequently revealed that he wrote the novel in a white hot rage, after he experienced similar events in his own life. Herzog is not necessarily Bellow, but there is a lot of Bellow in him. For what little it's worth, even the vowels in their surnames are the same.<br /><br /><b>A Natural Masterpiece</b><br /><br />What saves the novel from being a pure rant, is the introduction of Ramona. She is a business woman, as well as a mature aged student who has a degree in Art History from Columbia. She has also been a student in some of Herzog's classes.<br /><br />It's inevitable that a relationship between the two will develop during the course of the novel. They seem to be made for each other. There's a sense in which Ramona is an intellectual equal. However, she is also portrayed, like Madeleine, as extremely sexually attractive. Sometimes you can be both:<br /><br /><i>"Ramona truly was a desirable wife. She was understanding. Educated. Well situated in New York. Money. And sexually, a natural masterpiece. What breasts!"</i><br /><br />The interposition of this relationship into the narrative prevents it being too maudlin. However, to the extent that it reflects an actual relationship with a person who would become Bellow's third wife (even if the model is someone quite different altogether), it gives effect to an authorial desire for revenge on his second wife.<br /><br />As attractively as he portrays Madeleine in many parts, Bellow uses the fictional romance with Ramona to get over her and start a new life of even greater personal compatibility and sexual pleasure.<br /><br />There's an element of authorial immaturity in this entire construct. Any acquaintances of the Bellow family or circle of friends would have had no doubts about who and what was being portrayed in the novel.<br /><br />Yet, despite this apparent desire for revenge, the novel is one of the great literary works of the twentieth century. It's definitely one of my top ten, if not top five.<br /><br /><b>Herzog Comes to Bury Caesar</b><br /><br />While the breakdown of the relationship with Madeleine triggers the narrative, it is more inflamed by the dynamic of the relationship between Herzog and Ramona.<br /><br />Of course, in the manner of two highly flirtatious people who know what they want, the relationship is consummated quite early in the peace. However, Herzog is not sure he is ready for Ramona yet. He has too much on his mind. First, it seems, he has to get these things off his mind, hence the letters.<br /><br />Next, Herzog has to decide that this new relationship is what he really wants. Equally importantly, though, if the answer is yes, then, in the game of love, he has to play hard to get. For a little while, at least.<br /><br />Herzog is used to strong, if not dominant, women. Before starting another relationship, he has to ready himself for the challenge. He needs some grooming before he is ready to become Ramona's groom.<br /><br />As much as we know that Herzog could not possibly resist Ramona's sexual attraction, the process by which he gets to the liaison that will occur at the end of the novel is quite circuitous. Not to mention metaphysical.<br /><br />Herzog's initial instinct is to retreat into himself after the separation from Madeleine. In the same manner, he feels the temptation to escape Ramona's lure by running away to the relative isolation of his rural home in Massachusetts, from where he writes most of his letters.<br /><br />Of course, Herzog knows that he will eventually return to Ramona, as does she. The attraction is too great to turn his back on her permanently. He describes his temporary flight as like that of a runaway slave. He is still enslaved to the prospect of their love.<br /><br /><b>Turbulence of Spirit</b><br /><br />The narrative is fragmented and non-chronological, so we know about Ramona from early on.<br /><br />However, the structure of the narrative reflects the manner in which Herzog has been let loose on the world following his separation. If nothing else, he is suffering from <i>"irregularity and turbulence of spirit".</i><br /><br />The role of Herzog's letters is to come to grips with this turbulence. Ironically, for all the turmoil he finds himself in, <i>"though he still behaved oddly, he felt confident, cheerful, clairvoyant, and strong. He had fallen under a spell."</i> Despite his excess of nervous energy, <i>"he looked weirdly tranquil."</i><br /><br />Even though his letters focus on the past and his interior world, his attention has already moved to the future and his relationship with the external world.<br /><br /><b>My Story is History</b><br /><br />It's this change of focus that constitutes what is truly great and transcendent about the novel.<br /><br />Until now, the leonine version of Herzog has seen himself as part of the progress of History. He personalises all of the philosophy he has consumed and written about:<br /><br /><i>"One way or another the no doubt mad idea entered my mind that my own actions had historic importance, and this (fantasy?) made it appear that people who harmed me were interfering with an important experiment."</i><br /><br /><b>The Metaphysical Self</b><br /><br />Herzog's marriage breakdown represents a crisis in the progress of not just Herzog himself, but History as well. He endeavours to address it with the only tools known to him, his metaphysics.<br /><br />However, since the 18th century, metaphysics has become increasingly self-oriented, almost by definition.<br /><br />Littered throughout the novel are words deriving from the roots <i>"self"</i> and <i>"ego"</i>: self-consciousness, self-awareness, self-hatred, self-development, self-realisation, self-sufficiency, self-correction, self-obsession, egotism, ego-reinforcement.<br /><br />Herzog's plight is symbolic of what has happened to humanity in the last two centuries.<br /><br />He is <i>"aging, vain, terribly narcissistic, suffering without proper dignity."</i> He has lost his nobility. He has re-joined the plebeian. To use Heidegger's term, he has fallen into the Quotidian.<br /><br /><b>Let Me Look at You</b><br /><br />Bellow is keenly observant in respect of the world around his protagonist. We know what the environment and people look like. Herzog is a keen gardener. Trees and birds are often described with pastoral delight: <i>"All the while, one corner of his mind remained open to the external world."</i> However, ultimately, Herzog realises that he has grown too far away from the external world.<br /><br />It takes family and friends to tell him:<br /><br /><i>"Don't get highfalutin. I'm talking facts, not shit...Who told you you were such a prince? Dreamy boy...You're a highbrow and [you] married a highbrow broad. Somewhere in every intellectual is a dumb prick. You guys can't answer your own questions."</i><br /><br />Herzog recalls his own father's attitude towards his arrogance and pretension:<br /><br /><i>"He could no longer bear the sight of me, that look of mine, the look of conceit or proud trouble. The elite look."</i><br /><br /><b>Not by Self Alone</b><br /><br />Of course, the impetus for Herzog to sort himself out derives from his sexual appetite for Ramona. How can a man in his state be fit for a <i>"mature, successful, laughing, sexual woman?"</i><br /><br />For all his intellectual and academic seriousness, he is still at heart a human being with human needs: <br /><br /><i>"Herzog had committed a sin of some kind against his own heart, while in pursuit of a grand synthesis...I wasted myself in stupid schemes, liberating my spirit."</i><br /><br />He realises that he could have become conscious of this truth a lot earlier, if he had just paid more attention to those around him. If we could only bother to look into the eyes of others, we would see how we look to them:<br /><br /><i>"Man liveth not by Self alone, but in his brother's face."</i><br /><br />Herzog realises for the first time that life is not just about the individual, the self, in isolation from others and the external world:<br /><br /><i>"I really believe that brotherhood is what makes a man human."</i><br /><br />His new version of humanism is founded on some kind of fraternity. A relationship with the other. A relationship with others.<br /><br /><b>Combatting the Void</b><br /><br />Characteristically, it's not enough that this analysis be restricted to Herzog alone. Herzog, if not Bellow as well, diagnoses the problem as one shared by the whole of Western Civilisation. Thus, Herzog's solution to his metaphysical problem ends up being equally metaphysical and equally applicable to others.<br /><br />Herzog rails against various nihilist philosophies of disintegration and annihilation that he describes as <i>"the mire of post-Renaissance, post-humanistic, post-Cartesian dissolution, next door to the Void"</i> (we might recognise the cultural manifestation as Post-Modernism):<br /><br /><i>"What is the philosophy of this generation? Not God is dead, that point was passed long ago. Perhaps it should be stated Death is God. This generation thinks - and this is its thought of thoughts - that nothing faithful, vulnerable, fragile can be durable or have any true power. Death waits for these things as a cement floor waits for a dropping light bulb...History is the history of cruelty, not love, as soft men think. We have experimented with every human capacity to see which is strong and admirable and have shown that none is. There is only impracticality. [This is] the victory of death, not of rationality, not of rational faith...<br /><br />"The question of death offers us the interesting alternatives of disintegrating ourselves by our own wills in proof of our 'freedom', or the acknowledging that we owe a human life to this waking spell of existence, regardless of the void. (After all, we have no positive knowledge of that void.)"</i><br /><br />Herzog believes that life is too important to be abandoned to nihilism:<br /><br /><i>"We mustn't forget how quickly the visions of genius become the canned goods of the intellectual. The canned sauerkraut of Spengler's 'Prussian Socialism', the commonplace of the Wasteland outlook, the cheap mental stimulants of Alienation, the cant and rant of pipsqueaks about Inauthenticity and Forlornness. I can't accept this foolish dreariness. We are talking about the whole life of mankind. The subject is too great, too deep for such weakness, cowardice...a merely aesthetic critique of modern history! After the wars and mass killings!"<br /><br />"...Nietzsche: you speak of the power of the Dionysian spirit to endure the sight of the Terrible, the Questionable, to allow itself the luxury of Destruction, to witness Decomposition, Hideousness, Evil...You want to make us able to live with the void."</i><br /><br />Ultimately, Herzog argues that only humanism can combat the void, if there is such a thing. Man's backbone must hold us above and beyond entropy, at least temporarily. Whatever the concerns about rationality, technology, evil, abuse of power, they can only be addressed at an individual or collective level. There is no point in surrendering to the void.<br /><br /><b>Metaphysical Pleasure</b><br /><br />These metaphysical conclusions have cleaned Herzog's slate, and left him ready for Ramona. What awaits him is:<br /><br /><i>"not simple pleasure but metaphysical, transcendent pleasure - pleasure which answered the riddle of human existence. That was Ramona - no mere sensualist, but a theoretician, almost a priestess...<br /><br />"Ramona had passed through the hell of profligacy and attained the seriousness of pleasure. For when will we civilised beings become really serious? Said Kierkegaard. Only when we have known hell through and through. Without this, hedonism and frivolity will diffuse hell through all our days. Ramona, however, does not believe in any sin but the sin against the body, for her the true and only temple of the spirit."</i><br /><br />Now that Herzog, too, has been to hell and back, it's time that the two Orphic travellers met up again.<br /><br /><b>Kiss Me Again</b><br /><br />All his life, Herzog has been dogged by words:<br /><br /><i>"What can thoughtful people and humanists do but struggle towards suitable words?...I've been writing letters helter-skelter in all directions. More words. I go after reality with language. Perhaps I'd like to change it all into language..."</i><br /><br />By the end of the novel, Herzog has run out of words:<br /><br /><i>"At this time he had no messages for anyone. Nothing. Not a single word."</i><br /><br />No words are necessary, because he has discovered reality, and reality is enough. He is about to reunite with Ramona in his home in the Berkshires. A nice meal is in the oven. He has chilled a few bottles of white wine in the mountain spring. He looks at his watch. She will be coming soon. So, too, will he.<br /><br /><br /><b>SOUNDTRACK:</b><br /><br /><b>Bettie Serveert - "Roadmovies"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=VjQ7xeCijlE">https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=VjQ7xeC...</a><br /><br /><b>Bettie Serveert - "Certainlie"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://m.youtube.com/watch?v=hDDHJugkG1U">http://m.youtube.com/watch?v=hDDHJugkG1U</a><br /><br /><b>Bettie Serveert - "I'll Keep It With Mine"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://m.youtube.com/watch?v=u83Qy1FBahI">http://m.youtube.com/watch?v=u83Qy1FBahI</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><b>In Her Brother's Face (A Bright New Star Called Emmanuelle)<br />[For Rupert's Little Sister]<br />[After a Story by Saul Bellow/Moses Herzog]</b><br /><br />All over Rupert's freckled face,<br />Nature traced out constellations,<br />With every star in its place,<br />Except for one that occupied <br />What was, 'til now, an empty space.<br /><br />Nobody knew what it was called,<br />Not even the astronomers.<br />The man they asked was very bald.<br />His name was Hiram Shpitalnik<br />And by this star he was enthralled.<br /><br />Though his beard reached to his feet<br />And he lived inside a hat box,<br />Hiram had to accept defeat.<br />Only his grandfather would know.<br />His wisdom was much more complete.<br /><br />He lived inside a walnut shell<br />And all his friends were bumble bees,<br />But just by looking, he could tell.<br />It was a new discovery<br />That Rupert named Emmanuelle.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4299226295275040458" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_132672198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132672198" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132672198?book_show_action=true">Nov 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=100">100</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=plain-old-fiction">plain-old-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=famous-books">famous-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132672198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13380886934605729524">Most of us have one big advantage over rich people and fictional characters when it comes to dealing with our personal issues. For example, look at Moses Herzog in this book. Herzog goes through an ugly divorce, and his circumstances allow him to wallow in his misery and behave erratically for months. I’m sure any of us in similar circumstances would like to put our lives on hold as we picked at our emotional scabs while ignoring our jobs and taking trips across Europe. <br/><br/>However, most of us don’t</span>
  <span id="freeText13380886934605729524" style="display:none">Most of us have one big advantage over rich people and fictional characters when it comes to dealing with our personal issues. For example, look at Moses Herzog in this book. Herzog goes through an ugly divorce, and his circumstances allow him to wallow in his misery and behave erratically for months. I’m sure any of us in similar circumstances would like to put our lives on hold as we picked at our emotional scabs while ignoring our jobs and taking trips across Europe. <br /><br />However, most of us don’t get that luxury. Those are usually the times when we can least afford to screw up so even though all you really want to do is hide under the covers or drink heavily or eat ice cream or drink heavily while eating ice cream under the covers, we gotta get up and go to work. And pay the bills. And do the laundry. And get the oil changed in the car.<br /><br />And that’s to our advantage. Because getting over something like a divorce means moving on, even if you’re faking it half the time. Eventually, you’re not faking it anymore, you are actually living your life, and that’s how you finally recover. <br /><br />Or you just completely lose your shit and end up getting stuck in endless loops inside your own head as you ping pong from one impulsive thought and whim to another until you’re completely unable to tell the good ideas from the bad. Like Moses Herzog. If he would have had to get off his ass and go back to work rather than mooching off his family then he might not have gone cuckoo for Coco-Puffs and come unglued while writing a series of bizarre letters to family, friends, celebrities and dead historical figures.<br /><br />Yes, I know that Saul Bellow was using Herzog to make a statement about how a modern man viewed his life and society in the ‘60s, and the writing is as good as his reputation. But I just couldn’t get into it, mainly because I wanted someone to give Moses a brisk slap and tell him to grow up and get over himself. I didn’t dislike the character, I actually felt bad for him. That just made me wish even more that Herzog could start pulling his life back together instead of indulging in his self-involved musings.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13380886934605729524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2139194068" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2139194068" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2139194068?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=nyc">nyc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=canada">canada</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2139194068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15113468328424170859">This is without a doubt my favorite book by Saul Bellow. I am not sure it will speak to everybody, but it certainly spoke to ne. It captures the world of an educated, liberal, East Coast professor. He goes by the name of Moses E. Herzog, and yes, he <b>is</b> from a Jewish family. He is having a midlife crisis, has just gone through a second divorce and is looking back on his life. He is writing letters to friends, relatives and public figures, some dead and some alive. But these letters are <b>NOT</b> sent a</span>
  <span id="freeText15113468328424170859" style="display:none">This is without a doubt my favorite book by Saul Bellow. I am not sure it will speak to everybody, but it certainly spoke to ne. It captures the world of an educated, liberal, East Coast professor. He goes by the name of Moses E. Herzog, and yes, he <b>is</b> from a Jewish family. He is having a midlife crisis, has just gone through a second divorce and is looking back on his life. He is writing letters to friends, relatives and public figures, some dead and some alive. But these letters are <b>NOT</b> sent and the further one goes the less do they even resemble letters! <u>What he is doing is reminiscing, sizing up his life, determining in what direction he will go next</u>. The year is 1964.<br /><br />I liked this book as much as I did because the East Coast of the 1950s and 1960s is familiar to me. Psychiatry, Martin Buber, and talk and opinions and talk and opinions about all that is happening around one feels natural to me. Everyone has a view on everything and one’s views <b>must</b> be expressed. What is described is the world of my parents and my own youth. Herzog spends time in New York City, Massachusetts, Montreal, Martha’s Vineyard and Chicago too. What was in the news, what people were talking about, how people dealt with life <b>then</b> is all <b>here</b>. <br /><br />I liked the progression of the novel—where it starts and where it ends. Herzog’s next step is not spelled out clearly, but in my view, this is clear. As Herzog looks back we share with him his disappointments and misgivings He sees failures both in himself and in others, but who doesn’t?! What we are seeing is life as it really is. At the end, I see <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[hope, at least a willingness to try anew <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. This was perfect for me. For me, this is how life really is.<br /><br />The audiobook is narrated by Malcolm Hillgartner. He captures wonderfully the type of people the book is about. I have given the narration four stars. <br /><br />I recommend this book to those who breathe NYC, who feel comfortable with American East Coast mannerisms, are of the liberal, academic bent, have Jewish connections and grew up in the 1940s, 50s or 60s. I think you will love it and feel a kinship with it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15113468328424170859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10636932023328250319" style="display:none">To all the people that watched my brave struggle with this book; I dedicate this review to you.<br />I have really mixed feelings about this one. Was it an absolute struggle to read? Did I fall asleep after a page or two many times? Was I wishing I was reading something else, something were things actually happened, like, I don't know say <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6611315.The_Dark_Desires_Of_the_Druids_III_Desert_and_Destiny" title="The Dark Desires Of the Druids III Desert and Destiny by Isabel Roman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dark Desires Of the Druids III: Desert and Destiny</a>? Did I think the opening line: "If I am out of my mind, it's all right with me, thought Moses Herzog" was the best opening line I've read in a while? Did I think it was exceptionally well written? Did I admire Bellow for going on for over 300 pages about absolutely nothing and still have people go gaga over it? Yes, yes, yes...<br /><br />Did I think that Bellow used the book as an excuse to show off his elloquence and eruditism? (Like some other authors, cough, Cortazar, cough). Yes. So you see, I am getting schizoprenia.<br />I am glad I have finished reading it. I think it's solely responsible for the fact I was sleeping for 10 hours a day for 3 weeks. God, I need to read something stupid now.</span>

<div id="review_137606688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137606688" />
    <a title="Charles Matthews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3010528-charles-matthews"><img alt="Charles Matthews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1260253736p2/3010528.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137606688?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Matthews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Matthews" href="/user/show/3010528-charles-matthews">Charles Matthews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137606688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16356109770726972334">Considering that it's a novel with nothing you could call a plot, <i>Herzog</i> is an inexhaustible book. It touches on elemental human relationships (sexual, familial, social) and spins off into lofty philosophical debates, reflections on civilization, on the meaning of death, and on the American experience. It tempts a reader into close analysis while at the same time mocking such analysis. Moses Herzog is at once the most meticulously observed of characters and the most impossible to grasp as a whol</span>
  <span id="freeText16356109770726972334" style="display:none">Considering that it's a novel with nothing you could call a plot, <i>Herzog</i> is an inexhaustible book. It touches on elemental human relationships (sexual, familial, social) and spins off into lofty philosophical debates, reflections on civilization, on the meaning of death, and on the American experience. It tempts a reader into close analysis while at the same time mocking such analysis. Moses Herzog is at once the most meticulously observed of characters and the most impossible to grasp as a whole.<br /><br />It's a third-person narrative whose voice is clearly that of the person whose story is being told. The device isn't used for a coy ironic distancing, as in <i>The Education of Henry Adams</i>, but rather it grows out of the kind of fractured consciousness that is Herzog's. He feels his plight -- the cuckolded husband -- and feels it deeply, but at the same time he wants to observe it from the outside, to watch himself in the act of being "a loving but bad father" to his children, "an ungrateful child" to his parents, "affectionate but remote" to his siblings. "With his friends, an egotist. With love, lazy. With brightness, dull. With power, passive. With his own soul, evasive." He is, in short, a character in a novel that he is writing about himself.<br /><br />Or, perhaps more appropriately, a letter he is writing to himself. One way of approaching <i>Herzog</i> is to treat it as an epistolary novel, in which Herzog, whose current intellectual pastime is to write letters to other people, is also writing this very long letter to himself about how he has wound up where he is. Which brings up a key problem: How do we know that Herzog is being honest with us -- or with himself? How do we know that his ex-wife, Madeleine, is the conniving shrew that he presents to us? Or that Valentine Gersbach is the oily opportunist that Herzog thinks him to be?<br /><br />The truth is, we don't. Other people in the novel seem less perturbed by Herzog's plight, by Madeleine's and Gersbach's actions, than he is. This includes Gersbach's wife, Phoebe, who claims that Valentine and Madeleine are not having an affair. Is Herzog self-deluded? Everyone thinks he's crazy, and he has come to accept the fact that he might be. He is certainly unstable. His career is in a shambles, and two failed marriages have left him so wary of women that he can't trust his own instincts when it comes to his latest amour, Ramona, who seems to accept him for what he is. And when he finally starts to take some sort of action, it's utterly foolish and self-destructive. He is saved from its potentially disastrous consequences by his own ineptness.<br /><br />Which is only to say that Herzog is like Leopold Bloom, a kind of Everyman. Like Bloom, he is a misfit who desperately wants to fit, a cuckold, a wanderer, a dreamer, and <i>Herzog</i>, like <i>Ulysses</i>, ends on a note that is positive enough to provoke hope, but also ambiguous enough to allow that hope to be dashed. Like the narrator of <i>In Search of Lost Time</i>, he is an intellectual with the potential to liberate himself from the past while at the same time so aware of his past that he becomes inextricably tied to it.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16356109770726972334" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1330122856" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
