              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17804193302454894775">This is impeccable writing of the highest order and a reading experience I simply will not forget.<br/>But it was never an enjoyable read, how could it be, as the main focal point Moses Herzog is such a depressing and bewildering figure to spend time with , dwelling on the past of failed marriages and writing letters to people that he never intends to send. As Moses is a writer and teacher Herzog does take a the philosophical approach with it's narrative, and Herzog himself is such a deeply drawn cha</span>
  <span id="freeText17804193302454894775" style="display:none">This is impeccable writing of the highest order and a reading experience I simply will not forget.<br />But it was never an enjoyable read, how could it be, as the main focal point Moses Herzog is such a depressing and bewildering figure to spend time with , dwelling on the past of failed marriages and writing letters to people that he never intends to send. As Moses is a writer and teacher Herzog does take a the philosophical approach with it's narrative, and Herzog himself is such a deeply drawn character that in the end you can't help take to your heart, similar to what John Williams done with Stoner.This does demand your full attention to get the most out of it, not the sort of book to tkae to the beach or read in a cafe, some of the time it's difficult to tell whether things are happening in the past or the present but this never spoils a single page. A masterpiece of fiction, and it's easy to see Bellow was a big influence on Authors such as Philip Roth and Richard Ford.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=nobel-authors">nobel-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=postmodernism">postmodernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2585926-stela?shelf=neo-classicism">neo-classicism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326822884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12769162235993487773"><br/><b>"Pray tell me, Sir, whose dog are you?"</b><br/><br/>What is the world for the intellectual? The playground of his ideas or the hell of his emotions? For Moses Hezog, a forty-seven-year old former Professor in a mid-life crisis it is certainly both. Recently gone through a messy divorce and the tragi-comedy of a marital triangle, the hero looks for the cathartic liberation from this emotional ballast in two ways: by writing letters to acquaintances and strangers, to the living and the dead, and by remembering</span>
  <span id="freeText12769162235993487773" style="display:none"><br /><b>"Pray tell me, Sir, whose dog are you?"</b><br /><br />What is the world for the intellectual? The playground of his ideas or the hell of his emotions? For Moses Hezog, a forty-seven-year old former Professor in a mid-life crisis it is certainly both. Recently gone through a messy divorce and the tragi-comedy of a marital triangle, the hero looks for the cathartic liberation from this emotional ballast in two ways: by writing letters to acquaintances and strangers, to the living and the dead, and by remembering the past. The result? A very exquisite mixture between epistolary and psychological novel intertwined with cleverly hidden intertextual dialogues, in a perfect narrative structure and a memorable collection of characters. A masterpiece signed Saul Bellow.<br /><br />The novel follows Herzog’s quest to make sense of the world either following Tolstoy’s belief – that freedom is personal and indifferent to historical limitations, or Hegel’s conception – that freedom begins with the knowledge of death, knowledge fed by history and memory.<br /><br />Therefore, the letters are not necessarily a way of communication (he never sends nor finishes them) they are a way of self understanding, Tolstoyan way: “I go after reality with language.” Thus, he keeps arguing with Spinoza whether the desire to exist is enough to lead to happiness, he feels like rejecting Nietzsche’s view of any present moment as a crisis, a fall from classical greatness on the principle that he had a Christian view of the history despite his accusation that Jesus Christ enslaved the world with his morality, and finally he finds a new interpretation of Kirkegaard’s belief that knowledge can be acquired only through hell by seeing suffering as a personal choice; not by playing at crisis, alienation, apocalypse and desperation, but as an antidote to illusion:<br /><br /><i>…people of powerful imagination, given to dreaming deeply and to raising up marvelous and self-sufficient fictions, turn to suffering sometimes to cut into their bliss, as people pinch themselves to feel awake.</i><br /><br />Together with Samuel Johnson, Herzog discovers that suffering can acquire an almost hedonistic quality:<br /><br /><i>Grief, Sir, is a species of idleness.</i><br /><br />If the letters are the intellectual dialogues with the world, memories are the emotional ones. Through personal history, this time in a Hegelian way, Herzog rebuilds his own image, since: “I am Herzog. I have to be that man. There is no one else to do it.” On these grounds he recalls all his “reality instructors”: his parents who taught him to love and to lose the loved one, his women who taught him that “not thinking is not necessarily fatal”, that is he can divorce intellect from emotion unpunished, his friends who taught him that generosity has sometimes an unbearable price tag. Two memorable Dostoyevskian figures emerge in all their contradictory splendour from this recollection: his second wife, Madeleine, who, according to Herzog, tried to steal his place in the world and his rival and former best friend Valentine Gersbach, who tried to become him, emulating his opinions and gestures. The only form of the self preservation, Herzog discovers, is detachment, so the final lesson the hero is gradually taught is the acceptance of death, be it physical or emotional:<br /><br /><i>And you, Gersbach, you’re welcome to Madeleine.<br /> Enjoy her – rejoice in her. You will not reach me through her, however. I know you sought me in her flesh. But I am no longer there.</i><br /><br />However. However. Which is the door to freedom – intellectual or emotional? Tolstoy or Hegel? For it is sure you cannot go through both at the same time, since they are rather opposite. Herzog clams up in the end, refusing either word or feeling, or simply refusing to tell. It‘s up to us to open whichever door we seem fit – for him and for ourselves, in a dignified answer to the mocking question of Longfellow’s dog at Kew: “Pray tell me, Sir, whose dog are you?”</span>
  <a data-text-id="12769162235993487773" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2201648003" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2201648003" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2201648003?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780670002009" href="/book/show/8522922-herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=university-library">university-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2201648003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3487093609216969143">Moses Elkanah Herzog must be one of the most maddening protagonists in literature, but at the same time you can’t help but love him. This was my second Bellow novel, and I found it a challenging read: I kept setting the book aside, then picking it up only to read another 10 or 20 pages before setting it aside again. There were definitely moments when I thought I wouldn’t make it to the end, but then Bellow would come out with an amazing line that convinced me to give his alter ego another try.<br/><br/>No</span>
  <span id="freeText3487093609216969143" style="display:none">Moses Elkanah Herzog must be one of the most maddening protagonists in literature, but at the same time you can’t help but love him. This was my second Bellow novel, and I found it a challenging read: I kept setting the book aside, then picking it up only to read another 10 or 20 pages before setting it aside again. There were definitely moments when I thought I wouldn’t make it to the end, but then Bellow would come out with an amazing line that convinced me to give his alter ego another try.<br /><br />Not a whole lot happens in this novel, and although it’s narrated in the third person it sticks so closely to Herzog’s psyche that you could swear it’s actually first person, we’re so deep into his thoughts and his voice. He’s a former assistant professor with two ex-wives, a couple of kids, and a girlfriend. He hops between New York City, Chicago (where ex-wife #2 is living with the friend who cuckolded him, Valentine Gersbach, a one-legged redhead), and his home in the Berkshires. The whole time, he’s venting his spleen by writing (presumably imaginary? though perhaps they are sometimes written down too) letters to family members, ex-wives, and public figures (e.g. Heidegger and General Eisenhower). He also drifts into memories of growing up in Montreal, and lovers past and present. From what I’ve read about Bellow in James Atlas’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1927705912" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shadow in the Garden</a>, this is all highly autobiographical.<br /><br />It would be easy to dismiss Bellow and his fellows (like Philip Roth and John Updike) as sex-crazed misogynists*, and I know many feminists do, but from what I’ve read so far I think they’re actually just plain fun to read, and this type of voice, though perhaps overprivileged thus far in literature, is one that deserves to be heard. Bellow is showing us what it’s like to be in this kind of existence, and I value that. Plus, the man could <i>write</i> – alliteration, wordplay, comic scenes, you name it.<br /><br />*I only marked one line that struck me as misogynistic: “the terrifying menstrual ice of her rages.” That’s Moses’s mental description.<br /><br /><br /><b>Some favorite lines:</b><br /><br />“Better puritan restraint than the exhibition of pitiful puckered knees and varicose veins, pelican bellies and the indecency of haggard faces under sporty caps.”<br /><br />“<i>O Lord!</i> he concluded, <i>forgive all these trespasses. Lead me not into Penn Station</i>.”<br /><br />“Herzog was fairly deadly in polemics. His polite formulas often carried much spleen. His docile ways, his modest conduct – he didn’t deceive himself. The certainty of being right, a flow of power, rose in his bowels and burned in his legs.”<br /><br />“I brought all this on myself by telling Ramona the story of my life – how I rose from humble origins to complete disaster.”<br /><br />“Three thousand million human beings exist, each with some possessions, each a microcosmos, each infinitely precious, each with a peculiar treasure. There is a distant garden where curious objects grow, and there, in a lovely dusk of green, the heart of Moses E. Herzog hangs like a peach.”<br /><br />“Unexpected intrusions of beauty. This is what life is.” [admiring the whiteness of a sink!]</span>
  <a data-text-id="3487093609216969143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2151181289" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2151181289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2083814049269084155">I believe this is the third Saul Bellow book, that I have read, after Augie March and Henderson.<br/><br/>Say what you might about the man and his stories, but of all the great writers that this country produces, Saul Bellow, remains among its’ top tier.<br/><br/>Few can write as well as he can.<br/><br/>The story is about his doppelganger, Moses Herzog, originally from Canada, with much of his life spent in Chicago and New York, the Berkshires.<br/><br/>It takes place during the mid-nineteen sixties. Herzog is a good-looking man and </span>
  <span id="freeText2083814049269084155" style="display:none">I believe this is the third Saul Bellow book, that I have read, after Augie March and Henderson.<br /><br />Say what you might about the man and his stories, but of all the great writers that this country produces, Saul Bellow, remains among its’ top tier.<br /><br />Few can write as well as he can.<br /><br />The story is about his doppelganger, Moses Herzog, originally from Canada, with much of his life spent in Chicago and New York, the Berkshires.<br /><br />It takes place during the mid-nineteen sixties. Herzog is a good-looking man and an author and a college Professor.<br /><br />He understands that intellect does not provide liberty or clarity, rather it becomes a deceptively challenging and solitary cell.<br /> <br />Chasing skirts becomes his drug of choice, if only to temporarily disconnect himself from permanent sagacity, a chronic disease not easily remedied by medication or private interludes with intelligent and independent women, as is his wont.<br /><br />This is as much a story of Mr. Bellow’s life as it is of Herzog. Married and divorced four times, with female admirers throughout his academic career, you may decipher the context and predicaments of this novel.<br /><br />His writing is to be as admired as much as the hung products of one who uses a brush extraordinarily to communicate with future museum goers.<br /><br />He is not a one-trick-pony and much of his literary production covers different aspects of the mind, person, and the outlook of his characters, all disparate from Herzog, and so far, all revealing another aspect of this brilliant writer’s mind.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1752151245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1752151245" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1752151245?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who want to start an annoying argument about the Canon</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1752151245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11666932317654039450">For a while Saul Bellow seemed poised to become one of the 20th century's most famous authors, but he seems to have faded into the second tier nowadays. He doesn't have the visceral power of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/411437474' title='My review of Grapes of Wrath' rel='nofollow noopener'>Steinbeck,</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431570975' title='My review of Native Son' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wright</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1388785832' title='My review of Giovanni's Room' rel='nofollow noopener'>Baldwin,</a> or the technical ambition of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329888260' title='My review of Sound & the Fury' rel='nofollow noopener'>Faulkner</a> or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1355857477' title='My pinniped thesis on Woolf' rel='nofollow noopener'>Woolf;</a> he just writes good books. Maybe that cost him. My reaction to Augie March was, well, there's certainly nothing wrong with this book, nor is it going to change my life. "That's a good book," I thought. "Moving on." <br/><br/>And now here's <i>H</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11666932317654039450" style="display:none">For a while Saul Bellow seemed poised to become one of the 20th century's most famous authors, but he seems to have faded into the second tier nowadays. He doesn't have the visceral power of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/411437474" title="My review of Grapes of Wrath" rel="nofollow noopener">Steinbeck,</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431570975" title="My review of Native Son" rel="nofollow noopener">Wright</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1388785832" title="My review of Giovanni's Room" rel="nofollow noopener">Baldwin,</a> or the technical ambition of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329888260" title="My review of Sound &amp; the Fury" rel="nofollow noopener">Faulkner</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1355857477" title="My pinniped thesis on Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Woolf;</a> he just writes good books. Maybe that cost him. My reaction to Augie March was, well, there's certainly nothing wrong with this book, nor is it going to change my life. "That's a good book," I thought. "Moving on." <br /><br />And now here's <i>Herzog,</i> Bellow's last classic and his final, most thorough statement, and it asked quite a bit more of me than I was prepared to give. If you've read other books by him you may not be prepared for this much heavy lifting. <br /><br />It reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/609469890" title="My review of the K2 of books" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses,</a> in fact, and not just because the name Moses Herzog (<a href="http://www.npr.org/2009/05/18/103846270/head-in-a-fog-reach-for-herzog" title="According to Jeff Eugenides, anyway" rel="nofollow noopener">hurts-og</a>) was lifted from chapter 12 of that book. The talking, the constant trying to communicate: Bellow said it's about "the imprisonment of the individual in a shameful and impotent privacy." "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417056546" title="My review of Howards End" rel="nofollow noopener">Only connect,</a>" in other words, and that's what Leopold Bloom spent much of Ulysses trying to do. And the esoterical references, the mixing of viewpoints (first and third), the focus on mundane matters - Herzog and Leopold Bloom both spend a lot of time in the bathroom. Bellow himself, a realist, was "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1087282304" title="according to Michael Schmidt in Ch. 45 of Novel: Biography, reviewed here" rel="nofollow noopener">impatient with modernism,</a>" but it's hard not to see its influence here. It's not <i>as</i> difficult as Ulysses, but you might get the sense that it's Bellow's response to it.<br /><br />The book operates on three layers, switching deftly and rapidly between them. In the present, Herzog takes a short trip; has a date with a woman he's considering marrying; visits his ex-wife. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And considers shooting her and her lover, his former best friend. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> In the second, he flashes back along his life, his previous two marriages, his ramshackle house in the country, his Casaubon-esque career. (Sidenote: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182270038" title="My review of Middlemarch" rel="nofollow noopener">Casaubon</a> sure does pop up a lot in literature, huh?) And in the third, he writes a multitude of letters - to friends and newspapers and Schrödinger and Nietzsche - expounding on his philosophy of life. (And a lot of talk about like Kant and Hegel and shit, and I don't know anything about any of that so if you want to unpack it you're on your own.) <br /><br />The letters are boring and opaque:<blockquote>Good is easily done by machines of production and transportation. Can virtue compete? New techniques are in themselves bien pensant and represent not only rationality but benevolence. Thus a crowd, a herd of bien pensants has been driven into nihilism, which, as is now well known, has Christian and moral roots and for its wildest frenzies offers a “constructive” rationale. (See Polyani, Herzog, et al.)</blockquote><br />This doesn't make any more sense in context. They'll taper off throughout the book, as Herzog works through his midlife crisis and pulls himself together.<br /><br />There are some beautiful thoughts here; Bellow is, if nothing else, a smart and gifted writer. "I thought I had entered into a secret understanding with life to spare me the worst," Herzog says. "A perfectly bourgeois idea." Me too!<br /><br />But I found the experience of reading it frustrating, and not rewarding enough. Books like this - dense books, full of thoughts and philosophies and tangents and flashbacks - they ask a lot of the reader. They ask not just to be read but absorbed, focused on, made a part of one's life. I didn't expect Herzog to be this big of a deal, and maybe if I'd been ready for it I would have been more responsive to it - but it's also true that if a book asks a great deal of a reader, a reader is more likely to say "No" to it. "No, you are not the book for me. I choose not to commit as much of my brain to you as you demand." Your expectations for a summer romance are lower than your expectations for a long-term partner. These books are long-term partners, and many of them are not for us. We expect to have only a few long-term partners in our lives. Herzog will not be one of mine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11666932317654039450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_639917469" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/639917469" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/639917469?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=american-novels">american-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer639917469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7770116645082693010">This is rightly perceived to be a classic (4.5 stars), published in 1964. Written well before Bellow became the curmudgeonly conservative of his older age, when he attacked multiculturalism and post-modernism, it was a joy to read.<br/>It concerns the mid-life crisis of Moses Elkanah Herzog; when his second wife Madeleine elects to end their marriage and start a relationship with Herzog’s best friend Valentine. Moses writes letters to and about all those involved (letters that are never posted) and a</span>
  <span id="freeText7770116645082693010" style="display:none">This is rightly perceived to be a classic (4.5 stars), published in 1964. Written well before Bellow became the curmudgeonly conservative of his older age, when he attacked multiculturalism and post-modernism, it was a joy to read.<br />It concerns the mid-life crisis of Moses Elkanah Herzog; when his second wife Madeleine elects to end their marriage and start a relationship with Herzog’s best friend Valentine. Moses writes letters to and about all those involved (letters that are never posted) and also to significant political, religious and philosophical figures (current and historical). It is clearly a little autobiographical as there are many similarities between Herzog and Bellow, including the number of marriages they are on. Everything is told from Herzog’s point of view, so we don’t really know if the description of the end of the marriage is accurate or if Herzog is an unreliable narrator; although he is endearing, if not entirely stable. Herzog has insight into his plight and his own faults and has a good line in self ridicule. Despite the disintegration of Herzog’s life, the story is life-affirming and at times very funny.<br />It is, of course beautifully written and we are taken through all the labyrinthine meanderings of Herzog’s mind as he does sensible things, monumentally stupid things and begins to work through his problems in his own unique way. There are some odd notes; the character of Ramona springs to mind. She is the new woman in Herzog’s life (we are not sure how new). Herzog is a small time college professor in his late 40s. Ramona is younger, a marvellous cook, a voluptuous sex goddess, she loves Herzog unconditionally and is willing to save him and she is happy to put up with some of his less endearing habits (No wish fulfilment here Mr Bellow?). Of course the ex-wife Madeleine is a restless thinker and striver; more cerebral than Ramona. Sex goddess who cooks as well, in opposition to restless intellectual; what is he trying to suggest?<br />Despite the rather primitive male perspective, it is a great book and Herzog is rather likeable despite his incompetence and absurdity (or perhaps because of it).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7770116645082693010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_630624935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/630624935" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5650849-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310234599p2/5650849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/630624935?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer630624935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8073037993129932156">When I read this, I was living in the Berkshires not far from Herzog--or at least, not far from Norman Mailer in the summers. (Mailer would take his grandson-aged son to the Berkshire AA baseball games, in his Corvette.) Even closer than Herzog and Mailer lived two Russian BSO violinists and a Hungarian trombonist, in the summers. I improved my Russian through their aegis, but not my Hungarian. I was a college professor like Herzog, though not as sought-after by different institutions, nor yet a</span>
  <span id="freeText8073037993129932156" style="display:none">When I read this, I was living in the Berkshires not far from Herzog--or at least, not far from Norman Mailer in the summers. (Mailer would take his grandson-aged son to the Berkshire AA baseball games, in his Corvette.) Even closer than Herzog and Mailer lived two Russian BSO violinists and a Hungarian trombonist, in the summers. I improved my Russian through their aegis, but not my Hungarian. I was a college professor like Herzog, though not as sought-after by different institutions, nor yet abandoned by my wife. I was also, like Herzog, a compulsive letter writer; in fact, I had a monthly correspondence with one of the great letter writers, a Shakespearean at Amherst College who built the only Frank Lloyd Wright house in New England. His letters were eminently sane and funny; they drew out what sanity and humor dwelt in me. Or what I could muster to confront my own professional disaster and rebirth--through completing my 17C dissertation by use of the Chapin Rare Books Library miles away in Williamstown. My goal was to join the "English" profession in America--a paradoxical endeavor inherited from the role and decline of Latin education around the end of the 19thC. <br /> Herzog was indeed an admonitory epistle, a series of them in fact, a warning against my whole purpose. But I did not take it that way. Herzog was equally a warning against academic marriage, though I did not take it that way, either. And finally, Herzog provided a prototype of the joking sufferer, which I attempted to be in rising above my defeats. The fact that he was insane did not discourage me. Sanity might be over-rated. Name a famous leader who was sane: Hitler? Stalin? Napoleon? Attila? Caligula? Commodus? Caesar himself, reportedly killing 100,000 Tencterii in a week? Of course, few of these avatars of insanity could be called joking sufferers. Herzog, and possibly Bellow, were. And I aspired to be.<br /> Not Herzog, but one who wrote with Herzog's wit, a bit. After Henderson the Rain King, Herzog<br />was from a whole different tribe. One could not picture Herzog lifting a heavy stone and gaining a village-full of wives. Henderson had trouble satisfying his fifty wives, and Herzog had not satisfied his one.<br /> So I highly recommend this book to aspiring letter-writers and exiles from NYC recovering from marital and amorous defeats or from academic trials, to those who find insanity no bar, as they look around, to leadership and high office, or to those who discover humor in the midst of their suffering.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8073037993129932156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_384424023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/384424023" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/384424023?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer384424023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer610650677057431816">This is a tough one to evaluate. I guess Moses Herzog is a typical (Jewish) intellectual who absolutely can not cope with the ordinariness of life. After two divorces he is confronted with the harsh reality. He tries to fight his midlife crisis and the rising frenzy by writing dozens of letters to friends, but also to celebrities. After many wanderings he finds, through a new wife and the solitary contemplation of nature, more or less peace with himself and life. That's the basic story. Quite in</span>
  <span id="freeText610650677057431816" style="display:none">This is a tough one to evaluate. I guess Moses Herzog is a typical (Jewish) intellectual who absolutely can not cope with the ordinariness of life. After two divorces he is confronted with the harsh reality. He tries to fight his midlife crisis and the rising frenzy by writing dozens of letters to friends, but also to celebrities. After many wanderings he finds, through a new wife and the solitary contemplation of nature, more or less peace with himself and life. That's the basic story. Quite interesting and vividly told. As a reader you can really feel the struggle of this man.<br /><br />But, there's a but: Bellow has cut the story in hundreds of fragments: memories of childhood, flashbacks to the difficult relation Herzog had with women, hilarious incidents in the reality of today, letter clips, musings etc. And that makes the reading of this book very difficult and sometimes even hardly enjoyable.<br /><br />But Bellow goes deep, deeper than anyone. The story did captivate me because of the existentialist themes (just as in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9873.Dangling_Man" title="Dangling Man by Saul Bellow" rel="nofollow noopener">Dangling Man</a>), the ingenious confrontation between reality and fantasy, the descriptions of harsh city life and because of some really brilliant scenes (especially the lawyer characters are hilarious). But the story did also repel me because of the difficult grammatic constructions, the accumulation of very sour figments of Herzog (you are all the time in his head) and the very Woody Allen-like (my anachronism) atmosphere.<br /><br />Essentially Herzog can be seen as a remake of Joyce's Ulysses, including the final (carnal) liberation by a woman that stands firmly in reality, but Bellow does so in a unique way. Certainly a book to reread.</span>
  <a data-text-id="610650677057431816" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_153174480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153174480" />
    <a title="Mayra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1967563-mayra"><img alt="Mayra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605329000p2/1967563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153174480?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayra" href="/user/show/1967563-mayra">Mayra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1967563-mayra?shelf=abandoned">abandoned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1967563-mayra?shelf=what-a-letdown">what-a-letdown</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153174480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3314607964211653310">Novels without chapters bug me.<br/><i>Herzog</i> had some sort of dissection; sometimes the prose would be cut, only to be continued a couple spaces below. It still seemed vague and disorganized to me, having been for so long used to chapters and clear division.<br/><br/>I read a measly 18% of this book, and this is what I have to show for it:<br/>This book was extremely hard for me to review. I’m still not completely sure I have a finished opinion on it. It had been on my to-read list forever. And it’s long been on the </span>
  <span id="freeText3314607964211653310" style="display:none">Novels without chapters bug me.<br /><i>Herzog</i> had some sort of dissection; sometimes the prose would be cut, only to be continued a couple spaces below. It still seemed vague and disorganized to me, having been for so long used to chapters and clear division.<br /><br />I read a measly 18% of this book, and this is what I have to show for it:<br />This book was extremely hard for me to review. I’m still not completely sure I have a finished opinion on it. It had been on my to-read list forever. And it’s long been on the corner of my eye, beckoning, begging me to open it, and discover the amazing <b>Nobel Prize winner</b>.<br /><br />I thought I would love it. Sixty pages in, I still could not see the point the author was trying to sell with his story. It was not grabbing me. The introduction by Philip Roth had mentioned <b>Tolstoy</b> and compared their prose. I could not see the resemblance, as Anna Karenina is one of my all-time favorites.<br /><br />I felt <b>the writing was all over the place</b> and it was not able to focus on one sole idea for even the length of a couple of phrases. Perhaps that was meant to illustrate Herzog’s “madness”, but it was sort of annoying.<br /><br />Interspersed with that muddle were quotes of extreme genius, wit and introspection, so you might perhaps understand my difficulty in wrapping my mind on a fixed opinion.<br /><br />Throughout what I actually read, I could sense a dulled, dark humor underneath the drama of the plot. Most of the time I didn’t know if I should consider some passages funny or insulting; like this one:<br /><br /><i>“Will never understand what women want. What do they want? They eat green salad and drink human blood.”</i><br /><br />Nearing the 70th page, I grew extremely tired of the character’s crazy, chopped letter writing. Plus, my book was smelling all mildewy (thanks, <b>new</b> <i>Penguin Classics</i> copy, that managed to be the only book in my shelves – among 200 others – to get mildew/mold, having been less than 2 years old), so I just decided to abandon it for now, and maybe pick it up again in a couple of years. Maybe.<br /><br />I just honestly <b>could not bring myself to care for Herzog, or his incessant ranting</b>.<br /><br />These quotes are pretty killer though:<br /><i>“You have to fight for your life. That’s the chief condition on which you hold it.”</i><br /><br /><i>”People are dying – it is no metaphor – for lack of something real to carry home when day is done.”</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3314607964211653310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3480843211" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3480843211" />
    <a title="Andra " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92443064-andra"><img alt="Andra " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573429142p2/92443064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3480843211?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andra " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andra " href="/user/show/92443064-andra">Andra </a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3480843211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10565323468242012693">Thus far I am undecided. The plot is rather scattered and unstructured in a way that is vexing. I could understand how an unstable plot could contribute to creativity, but it does not appear to do so in this way. The protagonist of the novel is a writer, though Bellow has chosen to narrate in third person, thus excluding the writing abilities that could have been presented. This was an odd choice. The writing was not captivating, nor was it tasteful, and the plot was rather slow and mundane. Per</span>
  <span id="freeText10565323468242012693" style="display:none">Thus far I am undecided. The plot is rather scattered and unstructured in a way that is vexing. I could understand how an unstable plot could contribute to creativity, but it does not appear to do so in this way. The protagonist of the novel is a writer, though Bellow has chosen to narrate in third person, thus excluding the writing abilities that could have been presented. This was an odd choice. The writing was not captivating, nor was it tasteful, and the plot was rather slow and mundane. Perhaps Bellow's genius can be shown in another piece of his work, but I do not find it to be present in this one. However, I am eager to read more from him and further explore his literary world.</span>

<div id="review_543600153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/543600153" />
    <a title="Jan Rice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice"><img alt="Jan Rice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497584438p2/5864678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/543600153?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan Rice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan Rice" href="/user/show/5864678-jan-rice">Jan Rice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140072709" href="/book/show/1287187.Herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer543600153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13078514029172276427">The plot is slight. Basically, Moses Herzog, a 47 year old man living in the early 1960s in New York and Chicago, an academic, a grandson of immigrants, has done okay, as long as he was in a stable marriage while having affairs whenever opportunity and desire presented itself. He is not doing okay now, though, because he fell into love and infatuation and married the object of them, ditching the first wife. Now wife no. 2 has manipulated, used, and left him for a best friend. In the course of th</span>
  <span id="freeText13078514029172276427" style="display:none">The plot is slight. Basically, Moses Herzog, a 47 year old man living in the early 1960s in New York and Chicago, an academic, a grandson of immigrants, has done okay, as long as he was in a stable marriage while having affairs whenever opportunity and desire presented itself. He is not doing okay now, though, because he fell into love and infatuation and married the object of them, ditching the first wife. Now wife no. 2 has manipulated, used, and left him for a best friend. In the course of that, she persuaded him to buy a big house in the remote Berkshire mountains of Massachusetts. At her behest, he has among other things lost himself in fixing up that house at the expense of his professional direction. Of course she pretty much cleaned him out financially as well as emotionally and professionally. He's gone through what women often go through. Now he's the one who's bereft.<br /><br />Time to grow up and shut up--eventually.<br /><br />There is a climax and a denouement that I will not "spoil," but the plot is not the main thing here. It's the inner life, represented by a series of letters and dialogues Herzog enters into with the people in his life, past and present, and also with philosophers and other public figures, living or dead, whose ideas he has engaged with. Not real letters but his stream-of-consciousness as he blames, criticizes, argues, and rails against his circumstances, and he hits pay dirt, not to mention pithy quotes, early and often as he mines his crisis for nuggets of truth.<br /><br />Here's a Bellow quote I got from the Wikipedia entry on the novel: <i>"People don't realize how much they are in the grip of ideas", Bellow once wrote. "We live among ideas much more than we live in nature."</i><br /><br />Also I think the book confronts the problems of the times in the microcosm of this one individual, Moses Herzog. No longer does what once worked still work, in his life or in his society as a whole. On the surface it seemed he could do anything he wanted sexually and romantically. But free was not free. There were costs. <i>“Emancipation resulting in madness. Unlimited freedom to choose and play a tremendous variety of roles with a lot of coarse energy.”</i> ...Some sort of judicious choice required, some sort of order or rule needed. <br /><br />I can get Bellow's style in <i>Herzog</i> a little bit, because when I write, it's the words running through my head--the unfinished business, the issues that came up, the ideas, the memory of events still playing there--that I have to plug into. If I ever were to write fiction I'm afraid it would be related to this stream-of-consciousness stuff. But how one would plug some ideas into one character and some other ideas into another is a mystery! (Or decides on a plot.)<br /><br />I listened to the audiobook. The narrator's voice seemed dated, as though from the 1940s instead of the '60s. I thought he was mispronouncing some of the Hebrew words, when they came up, but when it was whole phrases, on which he had obviously received some instruction, I wasn't sure--until he pronounced "Here I am," <i>Hineni!</i> as <i>hie'-nee--nee</i>. It's what biblical figures answer when God calls for them, and should be <i>hee-ney'--nee</i>. Actually the first syllable is a "short i" sound, since it's not the accented syllable. ...Even so, the audio helps--I read it, didn't I?--as long as I have the back-up hard copy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13078514029172276427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1764791879" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1764791879" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1764791879?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143107675" href="/book/show/23281732-herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1764791879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11693417442217582132"><i>Untruth in her <br/>troth sallowed <br/>the language, sullied <br/>a certain conjugation: <br/>how she lied<br/>as she lay with me. <br/>Apparently her <br/>monogamy was too <br/>close to monotony.</i> <br/>From "Cuckoldom," B.J. Ward<br/><br/><i>"A man is only as good as what he loves."</i> Saul Bellow<br/><br/>While Herzog doesn't center on the misdeeds of Moses Herzog's second ex-wife and his former colleague/friend/her now-husband, Herzog's being shamelessly cuckolded by them has played a significant role in who Moses Herzog has come to be: an unstable college profe</span>
  <span id="freeText11693417442217582132" style="display:none"><i>Untruth in her <br />troth sallowed <br />the language, sullied <br />a certain conjugation: <br />how she lied<br />as she lay with me. <br />Apparently her <br />monogamy was too <br />close to monotony.</i> <br />From "Cuckoldom," B.J. Ward<br /><br /><i>"A man is only as good as what he loves."</i> Saul Bellow<br /><br />While Herzog doesn't center on the misdeeds of Moses Herzog's second ex-wife and his former colleague/friend/her now-husband, Herzog's being shamelessly cuckolded by them has played a significant role in who Moses Herzog has come to be: an unstable college professor who over-thinks day-to-day things, generally struggles with his inner wonk, still suffers the torment of betrayal and seems overly, and understandably, fearful of relationships with women, given the failures of his prior relationships and his female troubles. <br /><br />I chose the above quotes, I think, because recently I've unwittingly forced myself to confront the pain of cuckoldry and betrayal via fictional portrayals in a succession of novels I've read.<br /><br />As the novel opens, Moses Herzog is a hot mess. He has been humiliated in the worst way imaginable and just gone through a divorce with the manipulative hussy. He is on the fence on whether to continue with a new relationship with an attractive and vivacious woman who happens to suffer nymphomania (a slight exaggeration). Herzog is a neurotic, 47-year-old Jewish professor, twice married and divorced with two kids, one by each ex-wife. I found it interesting that Bellow obviously took the name of his protagonist from the Jewish merchant Moses Herzog who played a small role in Joyce's <u>Ulysses</u>, which I am just now reading. <br /><br />Herzog incessantly writes letter after letter after letter in a caffeinated romp over a variety of topics, all of which show his mental volatility. "<i>If I'm out of my mind, it's all right with me, thought Moses Herzog.</i>" Bellow involves the reader in a dissection of Herzog's mind as the unbalanced professor comes to acknowledge and accept his problems, realizing his intellectual elitism and cerebral clutter are a form of bondage on his soul that he must break to get in touch with the "<i>primordial person</i>" within him and thus "<i>achieve the experience of authentic being</i>." As the novel progresses, Bellow transitions from an epistolary novel to a linear structure to show how Herzog's mind healing.<br /><br />This surefooted, noetic examination of Moses Herzog--said to be Bellow's most autobiographical hero--is enjoyable and endearing. I must say that reading <u>Herzog</u> frayed my nerves a bit, though Bellow's other novels I've read have had the same effect.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11693417442217582132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_464299699" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/464299699" />
    <a title="Corey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8386063-corey"><img alt="Corey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528466470p2/8386063.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer7592085105489757883">Man, I just want to give Moses Herzog a hug. Any real review I could give would be a simple disservice to this wonderful novel, but this is one of the best stories I've read in a while, I genuinely didn't want it to be over as I was turning its last pages. Bellow's prose is beautiful, and I couldn't find a lazy sentence in the entire book. The characters are real and broken and loveable, and I will be sure to visit them again soon. </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3236507167" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3236507167" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3236507167?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3236507167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9042793478641071393">Rereading Herzog<br/><br/>I read Saul Bellow's National Book Award winning novel "Herzog" in the mid-1970s. I was in my mid-20s, had my first legal job, and wanted to keep my mind active beyond the practice of law. I remember the book appealed to me in its mix of lengthy philosophical reflection by its protagonist, Moses Herzog, together with Herzog's difficulty with sexuality.<br/><br/>After a long time of wanting to reread "Herzog", I took the opportunity to do so presented by staying at home through the pandemic</span>
  <span id="freeText9042793478641071393" style="display:none">Rereading Herzog<br /><br />I read Saul Bellow's National Book Award winning novel "Herzog" in the mid-1970s. I was in my mid-20s, had my first legal job, and wanted to keep my mind active beyond the practice of law. I remember the book appealed to me in its mix of lengthy philosophical reflection by its protagonist, Moses Herzog, together with Herzog's difficulty with sexuality.<br /><br />After a long time of wanting to reread "Herzog", I took the opportunity to do so presented by staying at home through the pandemic. The novel requires time to read as well as more experience than I had nearly 50 years ago.<br /><br />With its cerebral character and focus on one individual, the 47 year old Moses Herzog, the audience for this novel would appear to be limited. One can understand the appeal of the book to a certain stereotype of non-practicing educated American Jewish men, but it is valuable to see how Herzog's story and his what today are called "issues" have broader interest. In an early scene in the book, Herzog visits an elderly doctor for a physical, in large part to seek some reassurance about his mental state. (He also has consulted a psychiatrist). In a succinct reflection, discussing with the doctor his wife's betrayal, Herzog meditates on his divided self: "A strange heart. I myself can't account for it." The division pervades Herzog's story.<br /><br />The book tells the story of a twice divorced academic and intellectual who has been cuckolded in a most humiliating way by his second wife and by his apparent best friend. Herzog from his youth has been a ladies man, dependent on sex and on the approval of women in his many relationships.<br />He is devastated by the loss of his lovely, sexy, educated, but cold and domineering second wife. A highly introspective man, Herzog explores in the course of the short period of time in which this story takes place the nature of his many relationships with women. With all his intellect, Herzog knows that the physicality of a woman, her shape, her breasts, is important to him. He says of his lover, Ramona, who comes to play a large and positive role in the story.<br /><br />"She was short but had a full, substantial figure, a good round seat, firm breasts (all these things mattered to Herzog; he might think himself a moralist but the shape of a woman's breasts mattered greatly). Ramona was unsure of her chin but had confidence in her lovely throat, and so she held her head fairly high. She walked with quick efficiency, rapping her heels in energetic Castilian style. Herzog was intoxicated by this clatter. She entered a room provocatively, swaggering slightly, one hand touching her thigh, as though she carried a knife in her garter belt.'<br /><br />Together with Herzog's sexual experiences and urges and his interest in the sexuality and bodies of women -- which are described more sympathetically and with greater candor than they might be today--, the book concentrates on the protagonist's intellectual life. The author of a well-received book on Christianity and Romanticism, Herzog's academic productivity has come to a halt. Herzog uses his formidable intellect and reading to write letters to people, both famous and from his own life, which he never mails. The letters raise without resolving many issues including the nature of the self, the nature of American life, the tendency of intellectuals to denigrate the United States, and the need to find peace and meaning in life. The fame of this book is due in large part to Herzog's inveterate writing and accompanying brooding.<br /><br />The writing and scenes of the book also combine intellect and thought with street-wise colloquial writing in a way that mirrors Herzog himself. The book works because of its dense, closely-observed texture. It has settings in New York City, Chicago, and a small rural area in the Berkshires, which reminded me of the homes of Hawthorne and Melville. The acute observations and the detailed writing about places, streets, and people throughout this book are as important to the story as are the letter writing and reflection. Herzog and Bellow have an eye for their surroundings and an exuberance in their depiction. <br /><br />Herzog is at one and the same time a neurotically comical character, a man dependent on sexuality and on women, and a person who somehow tries to rise above it all in the search for wisdom. The book explores the tension in these characteristics and creates an inimitable individual. Moses Herzog tries to work to a degree of peace which allows him to live and to accept life and to come to terms with his intellect and with his physicality.<br /><br />With the advent of email and media, it is easier today, for better or worse, to share one's private thoughts with individuals and with a broad audience than it was for Herzog in 1964. I was glad to have the opportunity to get to know Moses Herzog better by revisiting his story and by thinking with greater understanding than I may have had years ago about both the demons and the ideals which drove him.<br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="9042793478641071393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/3961517-herzog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2011">2011</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152594484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1168157705618052343">I can't imagine what it would have been like to have read this when it first was published. The power, the storm, the frenzy of Bellow's words is amazing. It is almost impossible to compare him to another writer. I don't mean to say that he is a better writer than others. It is just hard to group or contain him. He isn't bounded by boundaries. Sure, he is a madman, but there are no institutions built to contain him.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_65845842" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65845842" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65845842?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/5326698-herzog">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Tata J</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      501 and 1001 Must Read Books
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65845842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12968422704373405386">This book was published in the year I was born: 1964. I will also always remember this book in the years to come because I read in while struggling with my jet lag here in Columbus, Ohio. I started reading this while in the stopover in Nagoya, Japan on my way here.<br/><br/>This book has won a number of accolades: National Book Award in 1965, All-TIME Magazine 100 Greatest Novels and listed both in the 501 and 1001 Must Read Books. It is said to be the major reason for Saul Bellow's bagging of the Nobel P</span>
  <span id="freeText12968422704373405386" style="display:none">This book was published in the year I was born: 1964. I will also always remember this book in the years to come because I read in while struggling with my jet lag here in Columbus, Ohio. I started reading this while in the stopover in Nagoya, Japan on my way here.<br /><br />This book has won a number of accolades: National Book Award in 1965, All-TIME Magazine 100 Greatest Novels and listed both in the 501 and 1001 Must Read Books. It is said to be the major reason for Saul Bellow's bagging of the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1976. <br /><br />What I like most about this book is its writing style. It is only in this book so far where the story is interspersed with the main protagonist thoughts via the letters (mostly unsent) he wrote and those covered varied trains of thoughts from political, scientific, sociological, psychological and even sexual. Moses E. Herzog (yes, the title of the novel is the surname of the main protagonist and his family) is a disturbed middle age professor who just came through his second divorce from Madeline. He is an absentee father not by choice but by circumstances. <br /><br />I could have given this book a perfect 5-star rating because I enjoyed reading this. However, I was not able to relate to the author and there were many references to the setting of the story (New York/Chicago in 1964) that I could not understand. It must also be the jetlag though.<br /><br />I am looking forward to reading Bellow's other works: The Adventures of Augie March and Humbolt's Gift. He seems to me like one of the American Masters of Literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12968422704373405386" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9vkwXtXZGfdZbpvOn5ewoYHCRHawnvrBQPsGPJkdcNiGLhQXdu0xePF6gga+6V3iDgO8W4A0UZ8DAbdSLN2D3Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mD1AE9QoJzaKyKSa3WIdaU5jyz4P5sjEr6x37JOCZAro6mRadxwPuSLcvVL8HPAqwaIzEz9MY5rsVsaCJkKXDw==')})
  }

First two robbers appear and hold him up; then things take a turn for the better when broken-nosed Frank Alpine becomes his assistant. But there are complications: Frank, whose reaction to Jews is ambivalent, falls in love with Helen Bober; at the same time he begins to steal from the store.Like Malamud’s best stories, this novel unerringly evokes an immigrant world of cramped circumstances and great expectations. Jairus Banaji\'s new work reaches back cent<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10501588076921997462\" style=\"display:none\">The rise of capitalism to global dominance is still largely associated - by both laypeople and Marxist historians - with the industrial capitalism that made its decisive breakthrough in 18th century Britain. Jairus Banaji\'s new work reaches back centuries and traverses vast distances to argue that this leap was preceded by a long era of distinct &quot;commercial capitalism&quot;, which reorganised labor and production on a world scale to a degree hitherto rarely appreciated. Rather than a picture centred solely on Europe, we enter a diverse and vibrant world. Banaji reveals the cantons of Muslim merchants trading in Guangzhou since the eighth century, the 3,000 European traders recorded in Alexandria in 1216, the Genoese, Venetians and Spanish Jews battling for commercial dominance of Constantinople and later Istanbul. We are left with a rich and global portrait of a world constantly in motion, tied together and increasingly dominated by a pre-industrial capitalism. The rise of Europe to world domination, in this view, has nothing to do with any unique genius, but rather a distinct fusion of commercial capitalism with state power.<\/span>\n  <a Vander has agreed to travel from California to meet her in Italy—in Turin, city of the most mysterious shroud—believing that he will have no difficulty rendering her harmless.But he is wrong.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow">About Saul Bellow</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1470607356p3/4391.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4391.Saul_Bellow">Saul Bellow
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,435
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5061133610257026125">Saul Bellow was born in Lachine, Quebec, a suburb of Montreal, in 1915, and was raised in Chicago. He attended the University of Chicago, received his Bachelor's degree from Northwestern University in 1937, with honors in sociology and anthropology, did graduate work at the University of Wisconsin, and served in the Merchant Marines during World War II.<br/><br/>Mr. Bellow's first novel, <i>Dangling Man</i>, was p</span>
  <span id="freeText5061133610257026125" style="display:none">Saul Bellow was born in Lachine, Quebec, a suburb of Montreal, in 1915, and was raised in Chicago. He attended the University of Chicago, received his Bachelor's degree from Northwestern University in 1937, with honors in sociology and anthropology, did graduate work at the University of Wisconsin, and served in the Merchant Marines during World War II.<br /><br />Mr. Bellow's first novel, <i>Dangling Man</i>, was published in 1944, and his second, <i>The Victim</i>, in 1947. In 1948 he was awarded a Guggenheim Fellowship and spent two years in Paris and traveling in Europe, where he began <i>The Adventures of Augie March,</i>, which won the National Book Award for fiction in 1954. Later books include <i>Seize The Day</i> (1956), <i>Henderson The Rain King</i> (1959), <i>Herzog</i> (1964), <i>Mosby's Memoirs and Other Stories</i> (1968), and <i>Mr. Sammler's Planet</i> (1970). <i>Humboldt's Gift</i> (1975), was awarded the Pulitzer Prize. Both Herzog and Mr. Sammler's Planet were awarded the National Book Award for fiction. Mr. Bellow's first non-fiction work, <i>To Jerusalem and Back: A Personal Account</i>, published on October 25,1976, is his personal and literary record of his sojourn in Israel during several months in 1975.<br /><br />In 1965 Mr. Bellow was awarded the International Literary Prize for Herzog, becoming the first American to receive the prize. In January 1968 the Republic of France awarded him the Croix de Chevalier des Arts et Lettres, the highest literary distinction awarded by that nation to non-citizens, and in March 1968 he received the B'nai B'rith Jewish Heritage Award for "excellence in Jewish literature". In November 1976 he was awarded the America's Democratic Legacy Award of the Anti-Defamation League of B'nai B'rith, the first time this award was made to a literary personage.<br /><br />A playwright as well as a novelist, Mr. Bellow was the author of <i>The Last Analysis</i> and of three short plays, collectively entitled <i>Under the Weather</i>, which were produced on Broadway in 1966. He contributed fiction to <i>Partisan Review, Playboy, Harper's Bazaar, The New Yorker, Esquire</i>, and to literary quarterlies. His criticism appeared in <i>The New York Times Book Review</i>, <i>Horizon, Encounter, The New Republic, The New Leader</i>, and elsewhere. During the 1967 Arab-lsraeli conflict, he served as a war correspondent for <i>Newsday</i>. He taught at Bard College, Princeton University, and the University of Minnesota, and was a member of the Committee on Social Thought at the University of Chicago.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5061133610257026125" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
